We can know by looking at the telemetry. It was 3.5 seconds of deceleration bringing the car speed down from 300 kph to about 120 kph. That's already at an average of almost 1.5G. It might seem that 2.4G is a little bit of a spike, but Hamilton had similar spikes in his deceleration, usually on downshifts. For example Verstappens highest deceleration happened about half a second before contact was made and that was from 151 kph down to 138 kph. At 2.4G it means that deceleration was only sustained for 0.15 seconds. That is not a brake test.  What is really curious is why Hamilton is lining up, almost onto the gearbox, right behind a car that is consistently and significantly decelerating? It is an insane move as it is almost impossible to synchronize the deceleration of a car that is downshifting from 8th gear back to 3rd gear. At no point did Hamilton drop below Verstappens velocity so he just kept creeping up closer until he got too close.  Just an incredibly silly incident, nothing in the data indicates malice. The thing is that stewards decide the fact a penalty is in place, but then also decide what the penalty will be. That makes it a lottery. A good change in the right direction would be to determine the penalty based on the incident.   Pushing off the track is 5 seconds if the other driver can rejoin. If the other driver cannot rejoin 10 sec penalty. With clear rules that decide when a driver is pushing another driver off the track it makes it clear. Then every other detail around the situation don't matter. Sure it will not eliminate discussion, but consequently used it will hopefully avoid the idea the FIA is pulling one team or driver ahead. About the position back on the restart: I think the deal was the right choice by Masi and the teams. Ocon getting between them made it complicated in normal conditions and the almost immediate red flag after the move happened essentially made it impossible to do it "the right way" and give the position back on track. Given they had to do something about it, I honestly think they  nailed it.  About the contact between Lewis and Max: Did Max do everything right and free of blame? No, the brake touch at the end was unnecesary. Was Lewis also playing games (Acknowledged it on press) and could have easily avoided contact if he didn't choose to basically park his front wing on Max's rear diffuser to not pass him? Yes.  Let's be honest: If Max didn't brake there and just waited for the next corner with Lewis there... Contact would likely have happened anyway. It's unfair to blame wholly Max when Lewis was not taking any kind of safety distance and got so close that any unexpected braking, car failure, twitch, etc on Max's side would have ended that way.   For me it's a racing incident, with a reprimand to each for their actions if you want to deal a penalty. The telemetry tells a very clear story if you are familiar with the art of brake checking. When executing a proper brake check there are a two fundamentals to pulling it off and causing the most disruption. First stab the brake suddenly and just ferociously enough to scare the other driver. Then you need to accelerate as rapidly as possible immediately after checking to avoid getting slammed into from behind. To do this you downshift another gear so that you get maximum acceleration after suddenly slowing. When executed properly it really pisses the guy off that gets brake checked.  If you look at Max‚Äôs telemetry you see that he downshifts to third right before hitting the brakes and then flooring the accelerator.  It‚Äôs a clear cut case of brake check 101.  I believe Max didn‚Äôt intend for Lewis to hit him which is why he checked then accelerated. Unfortunately for Max, at the exact moment he went for the check, Lewis had decided to overtake. Again this is clear from the telemetry. Lewis accelerated just before Max checked.    The FIA failed Motorsport by not punishing this act properly. I‚Äôm sure everyone in the room knew that it was a brake check, but exactly like Brazil, they don‚Äôt want to make a decision that reduces the spectacle they have orchestrated for this weekend.

Why isn't there a turn or time limit for giving a position back? For example, a team must notify a driver within 30 seconds from FIA notification, and a driver then has some number of turns (maybe somewhere between 4-6? Depending on track maybe?) to give the place back. Then you don't end up with a driver going most of the way through a track to a specific spot. It may make it more difficult to give a position back strategically, but if a driver recognizes an error and gives back immediately, or simply avoids action that would gain an unfair advantage off track, they'd avoid this whole situation entirely.
One thing that you didn't mention was Max's positioning of his car to let Lewis back in front. Max I believe parked his car in the middle of the track to force Lewis to scrub speed off by having to apply more steering angle to get around his car so Max would be able to stay closer to Lewis in the DRS zone. Effectively Max made his car a tighter chicane for Lewis to go around rather than move to one side where Lewis would have been able to keep the momentum down the straight (notwithstanding his brake test).
Brake Check? That's not even a thing in F1!!! I'm really peeved at the broadcast team for even introducing that concept. Any seasoned F1 fan is well aware that exploiting the "gray area" of the sporting regs IS a thing, and RB and Max exploit with the best of em. I will agree that Max was definitely choosing an interesting spot to let Lewis around per the stewards, and was probably as shocked as anyone that that car that had been on his gearbox the entire race drove up his back side instead of pulling aside and going around as LH had been given the "2.? G" open door to do so. Besides, and it's usually in NASCAR, but a "brake check" occurs between two cars with bumpers that are going relatively equal speeds and are within inches of each other, and the car in front is well aware that it's about to get hit from behind when he hits the brakes. Obviously Max was trying to inspire the pass early so that he could use DRS for an undercut attempt.....JUST LIKE HE DID AGAIN!! So, there was no "brake check" and desperate Lewis was caught drafting, when he should have been passing. Simple as that. As for Lewis "not leaving room to race" and forcing opponents off course to keep from crashing, just watch ANY race and you'll see him do it, and it's very calculated. Occasionally it's a lack of grip, but many times it's intentional car placement leaving the competitor with one option. Lewis had madd talent, doesn't need to play dirty to win, I sure wish he'd prove it....but he won't. Slowing from 304 to 108 kph would suggest significant braking over a length of time/distance, not just a single spike in braking the moment LH hit Max. The 69 bar of brake pressure is much more important, but the FIA did not say or show when in the braking process that level of brake occurred either.  2) Lewis was accelerating at the point of contact.  3) LH had 6-7 seconds to decide to go around Max, that's a long long time for a racing driver. The Mercedes was significantly quicker on the mediums and absolutely no team could get the softs to light up. It was here I noticed Verstappen concentrating on qualifying. The Red bull plan for the weekend was get pole, then try and keep the Mercedes behind for 50 laps. The thought, I guess, was with difficult overtaking and the very likely chances of Safety cars/red flags, Red Bull might just get a result.  Onto Saturday. The Red Bull was clearly running more downforce. The rear wing even had a Gurney flap thrown on. I thought at the time they are using more downforce to get the softs into the window quicker and this appeared to work quite well, as the quali runs in FP3 looked like a decent improvement but still not as quick as Hamilton on the mediums. Onto qualifying. Max's lap was over the limit but outstanding to watch. Coming into the last corner, I think the soft tyres, with the higher downforce, probably cooked the tyres a little, causing max to mis-judge the braking but ultimately it was lighting up the rears that sent him into the wall. So it looked like advantage Mercedes.  All routine for the start. The Mercs got away and it looked like a routine 1-2, even when Schumacher banged into the wall and they switched to the mediums, the Mercedes plan looked solid. Cut the corner, re-entered the track in a dangerous method. I guess the FIA gave him an option of dropping back 1 place to keep the championship alive. I'm ok with that. My initial thought was I bet those mediums will be screwed in 20 laps, after seeing the set that was on the car earlier. My thought proved correct. Hamilton was very quick through sectors 2 and 3. Verstappen owned sector 1 but was getting progressively slower as the cars entered the 30+laps phase of the race. Hamilton ultimately kept the gap very short through sector 1 on lap 34 then got on max's tail with full DRS.  Lap 35 overtake. Verstappen has continuously under braked when challenged all season and sorts it out when he finishes under steering or going sideways but he rarely stays on the track when he does it. Hamilton should have this move sussed by now, should brake harder and, when max is trying to sort himself out, take the corner with the old cutback move. I guess Hamilton is probably worried about the Torpedo move when Max re-enters the track. It is only racing when you are in control, stay on the track and avoid the collision. When max does this, its not racing as he is going for the gap without control and he has no intention of avoiding a collision. For sure, the rules need the ambiguity taken out of them, but the teams should know the rules and know to redress on their own volition so it doesn't go to the stewards and result in penalty. Maybe add extra penalty for not redressing - 5sec is often too little for cars that are 20sec ahead. Penalties keep the racing honest, and we should remember that. Although Lewis wasn't told Max was going to give back the position, and only had a limited amount of time to assess what was going on, I think he's at fault here - the reason why I've come to this conclusion is because of the course of action Hamilton took as soon as Max started to slow down.  So the key question here is: why did Lewis back off and not attempt a passing maneuver?  I have come up with several possible reasons, and please keep in mind that this is pure conjecture on my part.  Reason 1: Lewis was confused why Max was slowing down, and thought it could due to a VSC or Safety car.  Reason 2: He thought the Red Bull had suffered a mechanical failure, but wanted confirm weather or not there was a VSC or Safety car before passing.  Reason 3: He thought Max was up to something devious, and thought a passing maneuver was too risky.  Reason 4: Christian Horner's theory that Lewis wanted to make the pass after the DRS line.  Each of these reasons are the result of vigilance, indicating Lewis was aware of the dangers this situation presented - Therefor I believe Lewis must have known his decision not to pass created a higher risk of a collision and was negligent by not taking avoiding action sooner.     Opinions, thoughts and criticism welcome

One important aspect you miss is why Verstappen had such a bad restart. As usual, Hamilton was way further than 10 car lengths back. Under a safety car you are not allowed to leave such a gap, yet Hamilton routinely does. It is purely a tactic to cool down the tyres of those ahead of him. As this was a safety car restart Hamilton should have remained within 10 car lengths of Max at all times. Once the safety car pulled in Max was de facto safety car. Because Hamilton didn't obey the rules, despite reacting to the lights quicker than Hamilton, Max got a much worse start due to cooler tyres, leading to the incident.  If Mercedes are going to make espersions concerning driving standards perhaps they should examine those of their own drivers first.
